❓:}[}{[}The median to the hypotenusePythagorean Theorem (direct and inverse)Mean proportionals in a right triangleIn a right triangle A B Cleft(angle C=90^{circ}right), the height C D and the median C E are drawn. The areas of triangles A B C and C D E are 10 and 3, respectively. Find A B.

The areas of triangles ABC and CDE are 10 and 3, respectively. The median CE would be from point C to the midpoint of hypotenuse AB. In a right triangle, the median to the hypotenuse is half the hypotenuse. Wait, that's a property I remember. So CE = (1/2)AB. Maybe that will be useful later.Then there's the height CD. In a right triangle, the altitude to the hypotenuse has some interesting properties. For example, the length of the altitude CD can be calculated using the formula CD = (AC * BC)/AB. Also, the area of triangle ABC is (AC * BC)/2 = 10, so AC * BC = 20. That's one equation.Now, we need to relate this to the area of triangle CDE, which is 3. Let me try to figure out where points D and E are located. Similarly, depending on whether AD is less than or greater than AE.Alternatively, maybe DE = |AD - AE|? Let's see. If D is closer to A than E, then AD < AE, so DE = AE - AD. So depending on which leg is longer, the foot D will be closer to the shorter leg. Let's suppose that AC = b and BC = a. So if a > b, then DB = a^2 / c and AD = b^2 / c. Let's think.If E is the midpoint, then from A to E is c/2, and from E to B is c/2. If D is closer to B, then AD = b^2 / c, so the distance from A to D is b^2 / c. Therefore, AB=5√2 is correct.So the answer is 5√2, which in boxed form is boxed{5sqrt{2}}.Final Answerboxed{5sqrt{2}}</think>Given a right triangle (ABC) with (angle C = 90^circ), the height (CD) and the median (CE) are drawn. The areas of triangles (ABC) and (CDE) are 10 and 3, respectively. We need to find the length of the hypotenuse (AB).1. Area of Triangle (ABC): [ text{Area of } triangle ABC = frac{1}{2} times AC times BC = 10 implies AC times BC = 20 ]2. Length of the Altitude (CD): [ CD = frac{AC times BC}{AB} = frac{20}{AB} ]3. Median (CE): [ CE = frac{1}{2} AB ]4. Area of Triangle (CDE): The area of (triangle CDE) is given by (frac{1}{2} times DE times CD = 3). Using (CD = frac{20}{AB}): [ frac{1}{2} times DE times frac{20}{AB} = 3 implies DE times frac{20}{AB} = 6 implies DE = frac{3AB}{10} ]5. Coordinates and Distance (DE): Using coordinates, we find the coordinates of (D) and (E). For a right triangle with (C) at ((0,0)), (A) at ((0, b)), and (B) at ((a, 0)): - Midpoint (E) is (left(frac{a}{2}, frac{b}{2}right)). - Foot of the altitude (D) is (left(frac{ab^2}{a^2 + b^2}, frac{a^2b}{a^2 + b^2}right)).6. Calculating the Area of (triangle CDE): Using the determinant formula for the area: [ text{Area} = frac{1}{2} left| x_D y_E - x_E y_D right| ] Substituting the coordinates: [ text{Area} = frac{1}{2} left| left(frac{ab^2}{a^2 + b^2} times frac{b}{2}right) - left(frac{a}{2} times frac{a^2b}{a^2 + b^2}right) right| ] Simplifying, we find: [ left| frac{a b (b^2 - a^2)}{2(a^2 + b^2)} right| = 12 implies left| frac{(b^2 - a^2)}{a^2 + b^2} right| = frac{3}{5} ]7. Solving for (a) and (b): - Case 1: (b^2 - a^2 = frac{3}{5}(a^2 + b^2) implies b = 2a) - Case 2: (a^2 - b^2 = frac{3}{5}(a^2 + b^2) implies a = 2b)8. Hypotenuse (AB): - For both cases, (a times b = 20) leads to (a = sqrt{10}) and (b = 2sqrt{10}) or vice versa. - Hypotenuse (AB = sqrt{a^2 + b^2} = sqrt{50} = 5sqrt{2}).Thus, the length of (AB) is (boxed{5sqrt{2}}).,<think>Okay, so I need to find the length of AB in a right triangle ABC where angle C is 90 degrees. The areas of triangles (ABC) and (CDE) are 10 and 3, respectively. We need to find the length of (AB).1. Area of Triangle (ABC): - The area of (triangle ABC) is given by (frac{1}{2} times AC times BC = 10), so (AC times BC = 20).2. Median (CE): - The median to the hypotenuse (AB) is (CE = frac{AB}{2}).3. Height (CD): - The height (CD) from (C) to (AB) is given by (frac{AC times BC}{AB} = frac{20}{AB}).4. Area of Triangle (CDE): - Using coordinates and the determinant formula, the area of (triangle CDE) is calculated. The coordinates of (D) and (E) are derived, and the area is found using the determinant: [ text{Area} = frac{1}{2} left| x_D y_E - x_E y_D right| ] - After solving, it is found that the ratio of the legs (AC) and (BC) must be such that the area condition of (triangle CDE) is satisfied.5. Solving for (AB): - The legs (AC) and (BC) are found to be in the ratio (1:2) or (2:1). However, both cases lead to the same hypotenuse length when considering the product (AC times BC = 20). - The hypotenuse (AB) is calculated as (sqrt{(2sqrt{10})^2 + (sqrt{10})^2} = sqrt{50} = 5sqrt{2}).Thus, the length of (AB) is (boxed{5sqrt{2}}).]

🔑:D E: A B=S_{C D E}: S_{A B C}.## SolutionNotice that D E: A B=S_{C D E}: S_{A B C}=3: 10. Therefore, B C^{2}: A C^{2}=B D: A D=(5+3):(5-3)=4: 1, which means B C = 2 A C.Thus, A C^{2}=S_{A B C}=10, A B^{2}=5 A C^{2}=50.![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2024_05_06_a7c46f4bbe36942e4ae1g-32.jpg?height=260&width=440&top_left_y=2517&top_left_x=818)## Answer[The ratio of the areas of triangles with a common base or a common height]Problem

❓:5. Satisfy a>b>2 and make a b, frac{b}{a}, a-b, a+b can be arranged in a certain order to form a geometric sequence of real number pairs (a, b)(quad).(A) does not exist(B) exactly 1(C) exactly 2(D) infinitely many

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I have this problem here where I need to find pairs of real numbers (a, b) such that a > b > 2, and the four terms ab, b/a, a - b, a + b can be arranged in some order to form a geometric sequence. The options are that such pairs (a) don't exist, (b) exactly 1, (c) exactly 2, or (d) infinitely many. Let me try to work through this step by step.First, let me recall what a geometric sequence is. A geometric sequence is a sequence of numbers where each term after the first is found by multiplying the previous term by a constant called the common ratio. So, if these four terms can form a geometric sequence when arranged in some order, there must be some common ratio r such that each term is r times the previous one.Given that there are four terms, there are 4! = 24 possible orderings. But obviously, checking all 24 orderings isn't practical. Maybe there's a smarter way to approach this. Let's think about the properties of geometric sequences. If we denote the four terms as t1, t2, t3, t4 arranged in geometric sequence, then we have t2 = t1 * r, t3 = t1 * r^2, t4 = t1 * r^3. Also, the product of the first and fourth terms should equal the product of the second and third terms (since t1 * t4 = t1 * (t1 * r^3) = t1^2 * r^3 and t2 * t3 = (t1 * r) * (t1 * r^2) = t1^2 * r^3). Hmm, let's see. First, since there are absolute values, the function is always non-negative. The problem is asking for the maximum value, so I need to figure out the highest point this function can reach. Let me start by recalling that both |sin x| and |sin 2x| have periods of π and π/2 respectively. Wait, actually, sin x has a period of 2π, but when you take the absolute value, |sin x| has a period of π. Similarly, |sin 2x| will have a period of π/2 because the regular sin 2x has a period of π, and taking absolute value halves the period. So the function y is a combination of two periodic functions with different periods. The overall period of y should be the least common multiple (LCM) of π and π/2. The LCM of π and π/2 is π, right? Because π is a multiple of π/2 (specifically, twice π/2). So the function y has a period of π. That means I can focus on analyzing the function in the interval [0, π] and the behavior will repeat every π.So, to find the maximum, I can just look within [0, π]. Also, since both terms are absolute values, they are symmetric in certain ways. Maybe I can consider breaking the interval [0, π] into smaller intervals where the expressions inside the absolute values don't change sign. Let's think about that.First, let's consider sin x. In the interval [0, π], sin x is non-negative, so |sin x| = sin x. Similarly, sin 2x. Let's analyze where sin 2x is non-negative. The function sin 2x has a period of π, so in [0, π], it starts at 0, goes up to 1 at π/4, back to 0 at π/2, down to -1 at 3π/4, and back to 0 at π. So sin 2x is non-negative in [0, π/2] and non-positive in [π/2, π]. Therefore, |sin 2x| is equal to sin 2x in [0, π/2] and -sin 2x in [π/2, π].Therefore, we can split [0, π] into two intervals: [0, π/2] and [π/2, π]. In each interval, the expressions inside the absolute values have consistent signs, so we can remove the absolute values accordingly.So in [0, π/2], y = sin x + sin 2x. 🔑:5. frac{sqrt{414+66 sqrt{33}}}{16}Notice that y=|sin x|(1+2|cos x|), thenbegin{array}{l} y^{2}=sin ^{2} x(1+2|cos x|)^{2} =(1-|cos x|)(1+|cos x|)(1+2|cos x|)^{2} = frac{1}{mu lambda} cdot mu(1-|cos x|) cdot lambda(1+|cos x|) cdot (1+2|cos x|)(1+2|cos x|)(lambda, mu>0) leqslant frac{1}{mu}left[frac{mu(1-|cos x|)+lambda(1+|cos x|)+2(1+2|cos x|)}{4}right]^{4} = frac{1}{mu lambda}left[frac{mu+lambda+2+(lambda-mu+4)|cos x|}{4}right]^{4} . text { Let } lambda-mu+4=0, mu(1-|cos x|)=lambda(1+|cos x|)=1+2|cos x| .end{array}From equation (2), we know |cos x|=frac{lambda-1}{2-lambda}=frac{mu-1}{2+mu}.Combining with equation (1), we can solve for lambda=frac{sqrt{33}-3}{2}, mu=frac{sqrt{33}+5}{2}.Therefore, y^{2} leqslant frac{2}{sqrt{33}-3} times frac{2}{sqrt{33}+5}left(frac{sqrt{33}+3}{4}right)^{4}.Thus, y_{max }=frac{sqrt{414+66 sqrt{33}}}{16}.

❓:55 Find all natural numbers n(geqslant 2), such that there exist real numbers a_{1}, a_{2}, cdots, a_{n}, satisfyingleft{left|a_{i}-a_{j}right| mid 1 leqslant i<j leqslant nright}=left{1,2, cdots, frac{n(n-1)}{2}right} .

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I need to find all natural numbers n (where n is at least 2) such that there exist real numbers a₁, a₂, ..., aₙ with the property that the set of absolute differences |aᵢ - aⱼ| for all i < j is exactly the set {1, 2, ..., n(n-1)/2}. Hmm, interesting problem. Let me try to break this down.First, let's parse the question. For a given n ≥ 2, we need real numbers a₁ through aₙ such that when we take all pairwise absolute differences, they cover every integer from 1 up to the number of such differences, which is n(n-1)/2. So, for example, if n=2, the number of differences is 1, and we need the differences to be exactly {1}. That's straightforward. For n=3, the number of differences is 3, so we need the differences to be {1,2,3}. Then, as n increases, the number of required differences grows quadratically.But the problem is asking for which n ≥ 2 this is possible. So probably starting with small n and trying to see if such sequences exist, and then trying to find a pattern or a general rule.Let me start with n=2. Then we need two numbers a₁ and a₂ such that |a₁ - a₂| = 1. That's easy: set a₁ = 0 and a₂ = 1. So n=2 works.Next, n=3. We need three numbers such that their pairwise differences are 1, 2, and 3. Let's think. Suppose we have three numbers on the real line. Let's arrange them in order as a ≤ b ≤ c. Then the differences would be b - a, c - b, and c - a. These differences must be 1, 2, 3 in some order. So, the largest difference is c - a = 3. Then the other two differences must sum to 3. But the other two differences are b - a and c - b. So, their sum is (b - a) + (c - b) = c - a = 3. So, the two smaller differences must add up to the largest difference. But we need these smaller differences to be 1 and 2. Indeed, 1 + 2 = 3. So, that works. So if we set, say, a=0, b=1, c=3. Then the differences are 1, 2, 3. So n=3 works.Now n=4. Let's see. The number of differences is 4*3/2 = 6. So we need the differences to be exactly {1,2,3,4,5,6}. Let's try to arrange four numbers such that all these differences are present. Let's again arrange them in order: a ≤ b ≤ c ≤ d. Then the differences are b - a, c - a, d - a, c - b, d - b, d - c. These six differences need to be 1 through 6.The largest difference is d - a = 6. Then, the other differences must be the remaining numbers 1 through 5. Let's note that the next largest differences would be d - b and c - a. Let's see. Let me think of possible arrangements.Suppose we set a=0. Then d=6. Now, we need to place b and c between 0 and 6 such that the differences 1,2,3,4,5 are covered by the remaining differences: b - 0, c - 0, 6 - b, 6 - c, c - b.So, let's denote b and c such that 0 < b < c < 6. Then the differences are:- b, c, 6 - b, 6 - c, c - b.These five differences plus 6 must cover all numbers from 1 to 6. Let's see. Let's try to assign these differences.We need to cover 1,2,3,4,5. Let's think. If we can set the differences to include 1, 2, 3, 4, 5. Let's attempt.Suppose c - b = 1 (the smallest difference). Then we have one difference as 1. Then the remaining differences are b, c, 6 - b, 6 - c. These need to be 2,3,4,5.So let's set c - b =1. Then c = b +1. So:Differences:- b (from a to b)- c = b +1 (from a to c)- 6 - b (from b to d)- 6 - c = 6 - (b +1) = 5 - b (from c to d)- c - b =1 (from b to c)So, the differences we have are b, b +1, 6 - b, 5 - b, and 1. For ( n = 2 ): - We need two numbers whose difference is 1. This is easily achieved with ( a_1 = 0 ) and ( a_2 = 1 ). - Therefore, ( n = 2 ) works.2. For ( n = 3 ): - We need three numbers with differences 1, 2, and 3. An example is ( a_1 = -2 ), ( a_2 = 0 ), and ( a_3 = 1 ). - The differences are ( |0 - (-2)| = 2 ), ( |1 - (-2)| = 3 ), and ( |1 - 0| = 1 ). - Therefore, ( n = 3 ) works.3. For ( n = 4 ): - We need four numbers with differences 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, and 6. An example is ( a_1 = 0 ), ( a_2 = 1 ), ( a_3 = 4 ), and ( a_4 = 6 ). - The differences are ( 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6 ). - Therefore, ( n = 4 ) works.4. For ( n geq 5 ): - The number of required differences is ( frac{n(n-1)}{2} ), which grows quadratically. Constructing such a set of numbers becomes increasingly difficult and is proven impossible for ( n geq 5 ) due to the properties of perfect Golomb rulers, which are known to exist only for ( n leq 4 ).Thus, the natural numbers ( n ) that satisfy the given condition are ( n = 2, 3, ) and ( 4 ).[boxed{2}, boxed{3}, boxed{4}]]

The other numbers are all between a_{1} and a_{n}. Since there is a difference of frac{n(n-1)}{2}-1, then a_{1}+1 or a_{n}- 1 inleft{a_{2}, a_{3}, cdots, a_{n-1}right}=A. Suppose a_{2}=a_{1}+1. Since there is a difference of frac{n(n-1)}{2}-2, and a_{2}+1 notin A, a_{n}-1 notin A, then a_{n}-2 in A. Set a_{n-1}=a_{n}-2. Consider the difference frac{n(n-1)}{2} -4. Since a_{2}+1, a_{n-1}-2, a_{n-1}-1 notin A, then a_{2}+3 in A. Set a_{3}=a_{2}+3. At this point, a_{n-1}-a_{3}=frac{n(n-1)}{2}-6. Since a_{n-1}-1, a_{n-1}-2, a_{n-1}-3 notin A, there does not exist a distance of frac{n(n-1)}{2}-5. Therefore, n=2, 3, 4.
